<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Cardiovascular disease</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Class of diseases that involve the heart or blood vessels</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperlipidemia" title="Hyperlipidemia">high blood lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a><sup class="reference" class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">Aspirin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beta_blocker" title="Beta blocker">beta blockers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulant" title="Anticoagulant">blood thinners</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Deaths</th><td class="infobox-data">17.9 million / 32% (2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015De_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015De-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Cardiovascular disease</b> (<b>CVD</b>) is a class of diseases that involve the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> or <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> CVD includes <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">coronary artery diseases</a> (CAD) such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" title="Angina pectoris">angina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction</a> (commonly known as a heart attack).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Other CVDs include <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertensive_heart_disease" title="Hypertensive heart disease">hypertensive heart disease</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rheumatic_heart_disease" title="Rheumatic heart disease">rheumatic heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiomyopathy" title="Cardiomyopathy">cardiomyopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arrhythmia" title="Arrhythmia">abnormal heart rhythms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital_heart_disease" title="Congenital heart disease">congenital heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valvular_heart_disease" title="Valvular heart disease">valvular heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carditis" title="Carditis">carditis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aortic_aneurysm" title="Aortic aneurysm">aortic aneurysms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_artery_disease" title="Peripheral artery disease">peripheral artery disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">thromboembolic disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Venous_thrombosis" title="Venous thrombosis">venous thrombosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The underlying mechanisms vary depending on the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> It is estimated that dietary risk factors are associated with 53% of CVD deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Coronary artery disease, stroke, and peripheral artery disease involve <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> This may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a>, lack of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">exercise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypercholesterolaemia" title="Hypercholesterolaemia">high blood cholesterol</a>, poor diet, excessive <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcohol</a> consumption,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>  and poor <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-8">[8]</a></sup> among other things. High blood pressure is estimated to account for approximately 13% of CVD deaths, while tobacco accounts for 9%, diabetes 6%, lack of exercise 6%, and obesity 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Rheumatic heart disease may follow untreated <a href="/wiki/Streptococcal_pharyngitis" title="Streptococcal pharyngitis">strep throat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is estimated that up to 90% of CVD may be preventable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGill2008_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGill2008-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Prevention of CVD involves improving risk factors through: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Healthy_eating" title="Healthy eating">healthy eating</a>, exercise, avoidance of tobacco smoke and limiting alcohol intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Treating risk factors, such as high blood pressure, blood lipids and diabetes is also beneficial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Treating people who have strep throat with <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> can decrease the risk of rheumatic heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a> in people, who are otherwise healthy, is of unclear benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cardiovascular diseases are the <a href="/wiki/List_of_causes_of_death_by_rate" title="List of causes of death by rate">leading cause of death</a> worldwide except Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Together CVD resulted in 17.9 million deaths (32.1%) in 2015, up from 12.3 million (25.8%) in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015De_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015De-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-4">[4]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_standardized_deaths" title="Age standardized deaths">Deaths, at a given age</a>, from CVD are more common and have been increasing in much of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a>, while rates have declined in most of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_world" title="Developed world">developed world</a> since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOM2010_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-IOM2010-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Coronary artery disease and stroke account for 80% of CVD deaths in males and 75% of CVD deaths in females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-15"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Most cardiovascular disease affects older adults. In the United States 11% of people between 20 and 40 have CVD, while 37% between 40 and 60, 71% of people between 60 and 80, and 85% of people over 80 have CVD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Go2013_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Go2013-2">[2]</a></sup> The average age of death from coronary artery disease in the developed world is around 80, while it is around 68 in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOM2010_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-IOM2010-14">[14]</a></sup> CVD is typically diagnosed seven to ten years earlier in men than in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-16"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 48">: 48 </span></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/310px-Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/465px-Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/620px-Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inflammatory_heart_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">Disability-adjusted life year</a> for inflammatory heart diseases per 100,000 inhabitants in 2004<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Health_Organization_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Health_Organization-16">[16]</a></sup><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin refbegin-columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="column-count: 2;"> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b3b3b3; color:black;"> </span> No data</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff65; color:black;"> </span> Less than 70</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fff200; color:black;"> </span> 70–140</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffdc00; color:black;"> </span> 140–210</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc600; color:black;"> </span> 210–280</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffb000; color:black;"> </span> 280–350</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a00; color:black;"> </span> 350–420</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff8400; color:black;"> </span> 420–490</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff6e00; color:black;"> </span> 490–560</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff5800; color:black;"> </span> 560–630</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff4200; color:black;"> </span> 630–700</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff2c00; color:black;"> </span> 700–770</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#cb0000; color:white;"> </span> More than 770</div> </div></div></div></div>
<p>There are many cardiovascular diseases involving the blood vessels. They are known as <a href="/wiki/Vascular_disease" title="Vascular disease">vascular diseases</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">Coronary artery disease</a> (also known as coronary heart disease and ischemic heart disease)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peripheral_arterial_disease" title="Peripheral arterial disease">Peripheral arterial disease</a> – disease of blood vessels that supply blood to the arms and legs</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cerebrovascular_disease" title="Cerebrovascular disease">Cerebrovascular disease</a> – disease of blood vessels that supply blood to the brain (includes <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Renal_artery_stenosis" title="Renal artery stenosis">Renal artery stenosis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aortic_aneurysm" title="Aortic aneurysm">Aortic aneurysm</a></li></ul>
<p>There are also many cardiovascular diseases that involve the heart.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cardiomyopathy" title="Cardiomyopathy">Cardiomyopathy</a> – diseases of cardiac muscle</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hypertensive_heart_disease" title="Hypertensive heart disease">Hypertensive heart disease</a> – diseases of the heart secondary to high <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">Heart failure</a> - a clinical syndrome caused by the inability of the heart to supply sufficient blood to the tissues to meet their metabolic requirements</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_heart_disease" title="Pulmonary heart disease">Pulmonary heart disease</a> – a failure at the right side of the heart with respiratory system involvement</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiac_dysrhythmias" title="Cardiac dysrhythmias">Cardiac dysrhythmias</a> – abnormalities of heart rhythm</li>
<li>Inflammatory heart disease
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Endocarditis" title="Endocarditis">Endocarditis</a> – <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> of the inner layer of the heart, the <a href="/wiki/Endocardium" title="Endocardium">endocardium</a>. The structures most commonly involved are the <a href="/wiki/Heart_valve" title="Heart valve">heart valves</a>.</li>
<li>Inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cardiomegaly" title="Cardiomegaly">cardiomegaly</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Myocarditis" title="Myocarditis">Myocarditis</a> – inflammation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myocardium" title="Myocardium">myocardium</a>, the muscular part of the heart, caused most often by viral infection and less often by bacterial infections, certain medications, toxins, and autoimmune disorders. It is characterized in part by infiltration of the heart by <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocyte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monocyte" title="Monocyte">monocyte</a> types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_blood_cells" title="White blood cells">white blood cells</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eosinophilic_myocarditis" title="Eosinophilic myocarditis">Eosinophilic myocarditis</a> - inflammation of the myocardium caused by pathologically activated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eosinophils" title="Eosinophils">eosinophilic</a> white blood cells. This disorder differs from myocarditis in its causes and treatments.</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Valvular_heart_disease" title="Valvular heart disease">Valvular heart disease</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital_heart_disease" title="Congenital heart disease">Congenital heart disease</a> – heart structure malformations existing at birth</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rheumatic_heart_disease" title="Rheumatic heart disease">Rheumatic heart disease</a> – heart muscles and valves damage due to <a href="/wiki/Rheumatic_fever" title="Rheumatic fever">rheumatic fever</a> caused by  <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pyogenes" title="Streptococcus pyogenes">Streptococcus pyogenes</a></i> a <a href="/wiki/Group_A_streptococcal_infection" title="Group A streptococcal infection">group A streptococcal infection</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_factors">Risk factors</span></h2>
<p>There are many risk factors for heart diseases: age, sex, tobacco use, physical inactivity, <a href="/wiki/Non-alcoholic_fatty_liver_disease" title="Non-alcoholic fatty liver disease">non-alcoholic fatty liver disease</a>, excessive <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_(drug)" title="Alcohol (drug)">alcohol</a> consumption, unhealthy diet, obesity, genetic predisposition and family history of cardiovascular disease, raised blood pressure (<a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>), raised blood sugar (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a>), raised blood cholesterol (<a href="/wiki/Hyperlipidemia" title="Hyperlipidemia">hyperlipidemia</a>), undiagnosed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celiac_disease" title="Celiac disease">celiac disease</a>, psychosocial factors, poverty and low educational status, <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, and poor <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-17"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuster_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuster-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MichaMichas2012_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-MichaMichas2012-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CiaccioLewis2017_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-CiaccioLewis2017-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duell2022_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duell2022-21">[21]</a></sup> While the individual contribution of each risk factor varies between different communities or ethnic groups the overall contribution of these risk factors is very consistent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interheart_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-interheart-22">[22]</a></sup> Some of these risk factors, such as age, sex or family history/genetic predisposition, are immutable; however, many important cardiovascular risk factors are modifiable by lifestyle change, social change, drug treatment (for example prevention of hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and diabetes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McPhee2012_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-McPhee2012-23">[23]</a></sup> People with obesity are at increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Coronary_arteries" title="Coronary arteries">coronary arteries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<p>Cardiovascular disease in a person's parents increases their risk by ~3 fold,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and genetics is an important risk factor for cardiovascular diseases. Genetic cardiovascular disease can occur either as
a consequence of single variant (Mendelian) or polygenic influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-26">[26]</a></sup> There are more than 40 inherited cardiovascular disease that can be traced to a single disease-causing DNA variant, although these conditions are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-26">[26]</a></sup> Most common cardiovascular diseases are non-Mendelian and are thought to be due to hundreds or thousands of genetic variants (known as single nucleotide polymorphisms), each associated with a small effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacRae_2634–2639_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacRae_2634–2639-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age">Age</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calcified_Heart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1836" data-file-width="1951" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Calcified_Heart.jpg/220px-Calcified_Heart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Calcified_Heart.jpg/330px-Calcified_Heart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Calcified_Heart.jpg/440px-Calcified_Heart.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calcified_Heart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calcified heart of an older woman with cardiomegaly</div></div></div>
<p>Age is the most important risk factor in developing cardiovascular or heart diseases, with approximately a tripling of risk with each decade of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finegold_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finegold-29">[29]</a></sup>  Coronary fatty streaks can begin to form in adolescence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> It is estimated that 82 percent of people who die of coronary heart disease are 65 and older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Simultaneously, the risk of stroke doubles every decade after age 55.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Multiple explanations are proposed to explain why age increases the risk of cardiovascular/heart diseases. One of them relates to serum cholesterol level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jou99_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jou99-33">[33]</a></sup> In most populations, the serum total cholesterol level increases as age increases. In men, this increase levels off around age 45 to 50 years. In women, the increase continues sharply until age 60 to 65 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jou99_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jou99-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aging is also associated with changes in the mechanical and structural properties of the vascular wall, which leads to the loss of arterial elasticity and reduced arterial compliance and may subsequently lead to coronary artery disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated357_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated357-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex">Sex</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease_in_women" title="Cardiovascular disease in women">Cardiovascular disease in women</a></div>
<p>Men are at greater risk of heart disease than pre-menopausal women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finegold_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Finegold-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHF_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHF-35">[35]</a></sup> Once past <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a>, it has been argued that a woman's risk is similar to a man's<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHF_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHF-35">[35]</a></sup> although more recent data from the WHO and UN disputes this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finegold_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Finegold-29">[29]</a></sup> If a female has diabetes, she is more likely to develop heart disease than a male with diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coronary heart diseases are 2 to 5 times more common among middle-aged men than women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jou99_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jou99-33">[33]</a></sup>  In a study done by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, sex contributes to approximately 40% of the variation in sex ratios of coronary heart disease mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Another study reports similar results finding that sex differences explains nearly half the risk associated with cardiovascular diseases<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jou99_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jou99-33">[33]</a></sup> One of the proposed explanations for sex differences in cardiovascular diseases is hormonal difference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jou99_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jou99-33">[33]</a></sup>  Among women, estrogen is the predominant sex hormone. <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">Estrogen</a> may have protective effects on glucose metabolism and hemostatic system, and may have direct effect in improving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothelial" title="Endothelial">endothelial</a> cell function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jou99_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Jou99-33">[33]</a></sup>  The production of estrogen decreases after menopause, and this may change the female lipid metabolism toward a more atherogenic form by decreasing the <a href="/wiki/High-density_lipoprotein" title="High-density lipoprotein">HDL</a> cholesterol level while increasing LDL and total cholesterol levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jou99_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Jou99-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among men and women, there are differences in body weight, height, body fat distribution, heart rate, stroke volume, and arterial compliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated357_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated357-34">[34]</a></sup> In the very elderly, age-related large artery pulsatility and stiffness is more pronounced among women than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated357_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated357-34">[34]</a></sup> This may be caused by the women's smaller body size and arterial dimensions which are independent of menopause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated357_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated357-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tobacco">Tobacco</span></h3>
<p>Cigarettes are the major form of smoked tobacco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-18"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Risks to health from tobacco use result not only from direct consumption of tobacco, but also from exposure to second-hand smoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-19"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> Approximately 10% of cardiovascular disease is attributed to smoking;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-20"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup> however, people who quit smoking by age 30 have almost as low a risk of death as never smokers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_inactivity">Physical inactivity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">Sedentary lifestyle</a></div>
<p>Insufficient physical activity (defined as less than 5 x 30 minutes of moderate activity per week, or less than 3 x 20 minutes of vigorous activity per week) is currently the fourth leading risk factor for mortality worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-21"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>  In 2008, 31.3% of adults aged 15 or older (28.2% men and 34.4% women) were insufficiently physically active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-22"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>
The risk of ischemic heart disease and diabetes mellitus is reduced by almost a third in adults who participate in 150 minutes of moderate physical activity each week (or equivalent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrganizationUNAIDS2007_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-OrganizationUNAIDS2007-39">[39]</a></sup> In addition, physical activity assists weight loss and improves blood glucose control, blood pressure, lipid profile and insulin sensitivity. These effects may, at least in part, explain its cardiovascular benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-23"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturated_fat_and_cardiovascular_disease" title="Saturated fat and cardiovascular disease">Saturated fat and cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salt_and_cardiovascular_disease" title="Salt and cardiovascular disease">Salt and cardiovascular disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lipid_hypothesis" title="Lipid hypothesis">Lipid hypothesis</a></div>
<p>High dietary intakes of saturated fat, trans-fats and salt, and low intake of fruits, vegetables and fish are linked to cardiovascular risk, although whether all these associations indicate causes is disputed. The World Health Organization attributes approximately 1.7 million deaths worldwide to low fruit and vegetable consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-24"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>  Frequent consumption of high-energy foods, such as processed foods that are high in fats and sugars, promotes obesity and may increase cardiovascular risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-25"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>  The amount of dietary salt consumed may also be an important determinant of blood pressure levels and overall cardiovascular risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-26"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>  There is moderate quality evidence that reducing saturated fat intake for at least two years reduces the risk of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> High <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-fat" title="Trans-fat">trans-fat</a> intake has adverse effects on blood lipids and circulating inflammatory markers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BookerMann2008_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-BookerMann2008-41">[41]</a></sup> and elimination of trans-fat from diets has been widely advocated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RemigFranklin2010_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-RemigFranklin2010-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2018_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2018-43">[43]</a></sup> In 2018 the World Health Organization estimated that trans fats were the cause of more than half a million deaths per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2018_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2018-43">[43]</a></sup> There is evidence that higher consumption of sugar is associated with higher blood pressure and unfavorable blood lipids,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Te_MorengaHowatson2014_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Te_MorengaHowatson2014-44">[44]</a></sup> and sugar intake also increases the risk of diabetes mellitus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> High consumption of processed meats is associated with an increased risk of cardiovascular disease, possibly in part due to increased dietary salt intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MichaMichas2012_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-MichaMichas2012-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol">Alcohol</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_and_cardiovascular_disease" title="Alcohol and cardiovascular disease">Alcohol and cardiovascular disease</a></div>
<p>The relationship between alcohol consumption and cardiovascular disease is complex, and may depend on the amount of alcohol consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> There is a direct relationship between high levels of drinking alcohol and cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-27"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup>  Drinking at low levels without episodes of heavy drinking may be associated with a reduced risk of cardiovascular disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MukamalChen2010_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-MukamalChen2010-47">[47]</a></sup> but there is evidence that associations between moderate alcohol consumption and protection from stroke are non-causal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> At the population level, the health risks of drinking alcohol exceed any potential benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2011_3-28"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Organization2011_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Organization2011-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celiac_disease">Celiac disease</span></h3>
<p>Untreated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celiac_disease" title="Celiac disease">celiac disease</a> can cause the development of many types of cardiovascular diseases, most of which improve or resolve with a <a href="/wiki/Gluten-free_diet" title="Gluten-free diet">gluten-free diet</a> and intestinal healing. However, delays in recognition and diagnosis of celiac disease can cause irreversible heart damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CiaccioLewis2017_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-CiaccioLewis2017-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep">Sleep</span></h3>
<p>Not getting good <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a>, in amount or quality, is documented as increasing cardiovascular risk in both adults and teens. Recommendations suggest that Infants typically need 12 or more hours of sleep per day, adolescent at least eight or nine hours, and adults seven or eight. About one-third of adult Americans get less than the recommended seven hours of sleep per night, and in a study of teenagers, just 2.2 percent of those studied got enough sleep, many of whom did not get good quality sleep.  Studies have shown that short sleepers getting less than seven hours sleep per night have a 10 percent to 30 percent higher risk of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cespedes_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cespedes-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sleep disorders such as <a href="/wiki/Sleep_and_breathing#Sleep-disordered_breathing_(abnormal_sleep_and_breathing_or_sleep-related_breathing_disorders)" title="Sleep and breathing">sleep disordered breathing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, are also associated with a higher cardiometabolic risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid27647451_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27647451-51">[51]</a></sup>
An estimated 50 to 70 million Americans have <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sleep_apnea" title="Sleep apnea">sleep apnea</a> or other  chronic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleep_disorders" title="Sleep disorders">sleep disorders</a>.
</p><p>In addition, sleep research displays differences in race and class. Short sleep and poor sleep tend to be more frequently reported in ethnic minorities than in whites. African-Americans report experiencing short durations of sleep five times more often than whites, possibly as a result of social and environmental factors.  Black children and children living in disadvantaged neighborhoods have much higher rates of sleep apnea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socioeconomic_disadvantage">Socioeconomic disadvantage</span></h3>
<p>Cardiovascular disease affects low- and middle-income countries even more than high-income countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Di_Cesare_2013_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Di_Cesare_2013-52">[52]</a></sup> There is relatively little information regarding social patterns of cardiovascular disease within low- and middle-income countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Di_Cesare_2013_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Di_Cesare_2013-52">[52]</a></sup> but within high-income countries low income and low educational status are consistently associated with greater risk of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Policies that have resulted in increased socio-economic inequalities have been associated with greater subsequent socio-economic differences in cardiovascular disease<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Di_Cesare_2013_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Di_Cesare_2013-52">[52]</a></sup> implying a cause and effect relationship. Psychosocial factors, environmental exposures, health behaviours, and health-care access and quality contribute to socio-economic differentials in cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_2009_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_2009-54">[54]</a></sup> The Commission on Social Determinants of Health recommended that more equal distributions of power, wealth, education, housing, environmental factors, nutrition, and health care were needed to address inequalities in cardiovascular disease and non-communicable diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSDoH2008_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSDoH2008-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution">Air pollution</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" title="Particulate matter">Particulate matter</a> has been studied for its short- and long-term exposure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate_matter#Health_effects" title="Particulate matter">effects on cardiovascular disease</a>. Currently, airborne particles under 2.5 micrometers in diameter (PM<sub>2.5</sub>) are the major focus, in which gradients are used to determine CVD risk. Overall, long-term PM exposure increased rate of atherosclerosis and inflammation. In regards to short-term exposure (2 hours), every 25 μg/m<sup>3</sup> of PM<sub>2.5</sub> resulted in a 48% increase of CVD mortality risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOIthromres_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOIthromres-56">[56]</a></sup> In addition, after only 5 days of exposure, a rise in systolic (2.8 mmHg) and diastolic (2.7 mmHg) blood pressure occurred for every 10.5 μg/m<sup>3</sup> of PM<sub>2.5</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOIthromres_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-DOIthromres-56">[56]</a></sup>  Other research has implicated PM<sub>2.5</sub> in irregular heart rhythm, reduced heart rate variability (decreased vagal tone), and most notably heart failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOIthromres_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-DOIthromres-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doicirculationaha_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doicirculationaha-57">[57]</a></sup>  PM<sub>2.5</sub> is also linked to <a href="/wiki/Carotid_artery" title="Carotid artery">carotid artery</a> thickening and increased risk of acute myocardial infarction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOIthromres_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-DOIthromres-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doicirculationaha_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doicirculationaha-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiovascular_risk_assessment">Cardiovascular risk assessment</span></h3>
<p>Existing cardiovascular disease or a previous cardiovascular event, such as a heart attack or stroke, is the strongest predictor of a future cardiovascular event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tunstall-Pedoe2011_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tunstall-Pedoe2011-58">[58]</a></sup> Age, sex, smoking, blood pressure, blood lipids and diabetes are important predictors of future cardiovascular disease in people who are not known to have cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Organization2007_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Organization2007-59">[59]</a></sup> These measures, and sometimes others, may be combined into composite risk scores to estimate an individual's future risk of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tunstall-Pedoe2011_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tunstall-Pedoe2011-58">[58]</a></sup> Numerous risk scores exist although their respective merits are debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hernandezvan_Staa2014_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hernandezvan_Staa2014-60">[60]</a></sup> Other diagnostic tests and biomarkers remain under evaluation but currently these lack clear-cut evidence to support their routine use. They include family history, coronary artery <a href="/wiki/Calcification" title="Calcification">calcification</a> score, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_sensitivity_C-reactive_protein" title="High sensitivity C-reactive protein">high sensitivity C-reactive protein</a> (hs-CRP), <a href="/wiki/Ankle%E2%80%93brachial_pressure_index" title="Ankle–brachial pressure index">ankle–brachial pressure index</a>,  lipoprotein subclasses and particle concentration, lipoprotein(a), apolipoproteins A-I and B, <a href="/wiki/Fibrinogen" title="Fibrinogen">fibrinogen</a>, white blood cell count, <a href="/wiki/Homocysteine" title="Homocysteine">homocysteine</a>, N-terminal pro B-type natriuretic peptide  (NT-proBNP), and markers of kidney function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HlatkyGreenland2009_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-HlatkyGreenland2009-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EckelCornier2014_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-EckelCornier2014-62">[62]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_blood_phosphorus" title="High blood phosphorus">High blood phosphorus</a> is also linked to an increased risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Depression_and_traumatic_stress">Depression and traumatic stress</span></h3>
<p>There is evidence that mental health problems, in particular depression and traumatic stress, is linked to cardiovascular diseases. Whereas mental health problems are known to be associated with risk factors for cardiovascular diseases such as smoking, poor diet, and a sedentary lifestyle, these factors alone do not explain the increased risk of cardiovascular diseases seen in depression, stress, and anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Moreover, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">posttraumatic stress disorder</a> is independently associated with increased risk for incident coronary heart disease, even after adjusting for depression and other covariates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupational_exposure">Occupational exposure</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Occupational_cardiovascular_disease" title="Occupational cardiovascular disease">Occupational cardiovascular disease</a></div>
<p>Little is known about the relationship between work and cardiovascular disease, but links have been established between certain toxins, extreme heat and cold, exposure to tobacco smoke, and mental health concerns such as stress and depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-chemical_risk_factors">Non-chemical risk factors</span></h4>
<p>A 2015 SBU-report looking at non-chemical factors found an association for those:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>with mentally stressful work with a lack of control over their working situation — with an effort-reward imbalance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>who experience low social support at work; who experience injustice or experience insufficient opportunities for personal development; or those who experience job insecurity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>those who work night schedules; or have long working weeks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>those who are exposed to noise<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Specifically the risk of <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> was also increased by exposure to ionizing radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-5"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup> Hypertension develops more often in those who experience job strain and who have shift-work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-6"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup> Differences between women and men in risk are small, however men risk having and dying of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_attack" title="Heart attack">heart attacks</a> or stroke twice as often as women during working life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-7"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_risk_factors">Chemical risk factors</span></h4>
<p>A 2017 SBU report found evidence that workplace exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica_dust" title="Silica dust">silica dust</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engine_exhaust" title="Engine exhaust">engine exhaust</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welding_fumes" title="Welding fumes">welding fumes</a> is associated with heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2017_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2017-68">[68]</a></sup> Associations also exist for exposure to <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benzo(a)pyrene" title="Benzo(a)pyrene">benzopyrenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynamite" title="Dynamite">dynamite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulphide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cutting_fluid" title="Cutting fluid">metalworking fluids</a> and occupational exposure to <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoke" title="Tobacco smoke">tobacco smoke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2017_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2017-68">[68]</a></sup> Working  with  the  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrolytic" title="Electrolytic">electrolytic</a> production of  aluminium  or  the  production  of  paper  when  the  sulphate pulping  process  is  used  is  associated with heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2017_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2017-68">[68]</a></sup> An association was also found  between  heart  disease  and  exposure  to  compounds  which  are  no  longer  permitted  in certain  work  environments,  such  as  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenoxy_acid" title="Phenoxy acid">phenoxy acids</a> containing <a href="/wiki/2,3,7,8-Tetrachlorodibenzodioxin" title="2,3,7,8-Tetrachlorodibenzodioxin">TCDD</a>(dioxin) or <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2017_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2017-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Workplace exposure to silica dust or asbestos is also associated with <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_heart_disease" title="Pulmonary heart disease">pulmonary heart disease</a>. There is evidence that workplace  exposure to lead, carbon disulphide, phenoxyacids containing  TCDD, as well as working in an environment where aluminum is being electrolytically produced, is associated with <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SBU2017_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-SBU2017-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Somatic_mutations">Somatic mutations</span></h3>
<p>As of 2017, evidence suggests that certain <a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">leukemia</a>-associated <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a> in <a href="/wiki/Blood_cell" title="Blood cell">blood cells</a> may also lead to increased risk of cardiovascular disease. Several large-scale research projects looking at human genetic data have found a robust link between the presence of these mutations, a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Clonal_hematopoiesis" title="Clonal hematopoiesis">clonal hematopoiesis</a>, and cardiovascular disease-related incidents and mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_therapy">Radiation therapy</span></h3>
<p>Radiation treatments for cancer can increase the risk of heart disease and death, as observed in breast cancer therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>  Therapeutic radiation increases the risk of a subsequent heart attack or stroke by 1.5 to 4 times;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid:20298931_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid:20298931-71">[71]</a></sup> the increase depends on the dose strength, volume, and location.
</p><p>Side-effects from radiation therapy for cardiovascular diseases have been termed <i>radiation-induced heart disease</i> or <i>radiation-induced vascular disease</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid:28911261_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid:28911261-72">[72]</a></sup> Symptoms are dose-dependent and include <a href="/wiki/Cardiomyopathy" title="Cardiomyopathy">cardiomyopathy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myocardial_fibrosis" title="Myocardial fibrosis">myocardial fibrosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valvular_heart_disease" title="Valvular heart disease">valvular heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">coronary artery disease</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_arrhythmia" title="Heart arrhythmia">heart arrhythmia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_artery_disease" title="Peripheral artery disease">peripheral artery disease</a>. Radiation-induced fibrosis, vascular <a href="/wiki/Endothelial_dysfunction" title="Endothelial dysfunction">cell damage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a> can lead to these and other late side-effect symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid:28911261_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid:28911261-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cardiovascular_calcification_-_Sergio_Bertazzo.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Density-Dependent Colour Scanning Electron Micrograph SEM (DDC-SEM) of cardiovascular calcification, showing in orange calcium phosphate spherical particles (denser material) and, in green, the extracellular matrix (less dense material)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertazzo_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertazzo-73">[73]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Population-based studies show that atherosclerosis, the major precursor of cardiovascular disease, begins in childhood. The Pathobiological Determinants of Atherosclerosis in Youth (PDAY) study demonstrated that intimal lesions appear in all the aortas and more than half of the right coronary arteries of youths aged 7–9 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obesity and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a> are linked to cardiovascular disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> as are a history of chronic <a href="/wiki/Kidney_disease" title="Kidney disease">kidney disease</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypercholesterolaemia" title="Hypercholesterolaemia">hypercholesterolaemia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps01_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-nps01-76">[76]</a></sup>  In fact, cardiovascular disease is the most life-threatening of the diabetic complications and diabetics are two- to four-fold more likely to die of cardiovascular-related causes than nondiabetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Screening">Screening</span></h2>
<p>Screening <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ECG" title="ECG">ECGs</a> (either at rest or with exercise) are not recommended in those without symptoms who are at low risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> This includes those who are young without risk factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In those at higher risk the evidence for screening with ECGs is inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Annals2012_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Annals2012-82">[82]</a></sup> Additionally <a href="/wiki/Echocardiography" title="Echocardiography">echocardiography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_perfusion_imaging" title="Myocardial perfusion imaging">myocardial perfusion imaging</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_stress_test" title="Cardiac stress test">cardiac stress testing</a> is not recommended in those at low risk who do not have symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Biomarker_(medicine)" title="Biomarker (medicine)">biomarkers</a> may add to conventional cardiovascular risk factors in predicting the risk of future cardiovascular disease; however, the value of some biomarkers is questionable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ankle-brachial_index" title="Ankle-brachial index">Ankle-brachial index</a> (ABI), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-sensitivity_C-reactive_protein" title="High-sensitivity C-reactive protein">high-sensitivity C-reactive protein</a> (hsCRP), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_calcium" title="Coronary artery calcium">coronary artery calcium</a>, are also of unclear benefit in those without symptoms as of 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The NIH recommends lipid testing in children beginning at the age of 2 if there is a family history of heart disease or lipid problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> It is hoped that early testing will improve lifestyle factors in those at risk such as diet and exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Screening and selection for primary prevention interventions has traditionally been done through absolute risk using a variety of scores (ex. Framingham or Reynolds risk scores).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-89">[89]</a></sup> This stratification has separated people who receive the lifestyle interventions (generally lower and intermediate risk) from the medication (higher risk). The number and variety of risk scores available for use has multiplied, but their efficacy according to a 2016 review was unclear due to lack of external validation or impact analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Risk stratification models often lack sensitivity for population groups and do not account for the large number of negative events among the intermediate and low risk groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-89">[89]</a></sup> As a result, future preventative screening appears to shift toward applying prevention according to randomized trial results of each intervention rather than large-scale risk assessment.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<p>Up to 90% of cardiovascular disease may be preventable if established risk factors are avoided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGill2008_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGill2008-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Currently practised measures to prevent cardiovascular disease include:
</p>
<ul><li>Maintaining a <a href="/wiki/Healthy_diet" title="Healthy diet">healthy diet</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_diet" title="Mediterranean diet">Mediterranean diet</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegan</a> or another <a href="/wiki/Plant-based_diet" title="Plant-based diet">plant-based diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_Direct_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Direct-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>Replacing <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> with healthier choices: Clinical trials show that replacing saturated fat with polyunsaturated vegetable oil reduced CVD by 30%. Prospective observational studies show that in many populations lower intake of saturated fat coupled with higher intake of polyunsaturated and monounsaturated fat is associated with lower rates of CVD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>Decrease body fat if overweight or obese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The effect of weight loss is often difficult to distinguish from dietary change, and evidence on weight reducing diets is limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In observational studies of people with severe obesity, weight loss following bariatric surgery is associated with a 46% reduction in cardiovascular risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>Limit alcohol consumption to the recommended daily limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_Direct_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Direct-92">[92]</a></sup> People who moderately consume alcoholic drinks have a 25–30% lower risk of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mostofsky_979–987_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mostofsky_979–987-101">[101]</a></sup> However, people who are genetically predisposed to consume less alcohol have lower rates of cardiovascular disease<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> suggesting that alcohol itself may not be protective. Excessive alcohol intake increases the risk of cardiovascular disease<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mostofsky_979–987_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mostofsky_979–987-101">[101]</a></sup> and consumption of alcohol is associated with increased risk of a cardiovascular event in the day following consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mostofsky_979–987_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mostofsky_979–987-101">[101]</a></sup></li>
<li>Decrease non-<a href="/wiki/High-density_lipoprotein" title="High-density lipoprotein">HDL cholesterol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Statin" title="Statin">Statin</a> treatment reduces cardiovascular mortality by about 31%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Smoking_cessation" title="Smoking cessation">Stopping smoking</a> and avoidance of second-hand smoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_Direct_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Direct-92">[92]</a></sup> Stopping smoking reduces risk by about 35%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>At least 150 minutes (2 hours and 30 minutes) of moderate exercise per week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lower blood pressure, if elevated. A 10 mmHg reduction in blood pressure reduces risk by about 20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ettehad_957–967_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ettehad_957–967-110">[110]</a></sup> Lowering blood pressure appears to be effective even at normal blood pressure ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></li>
<li>Decrease <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" title="Stress (psychological)">psychosocial stress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> This measure may be complicated by imprecise definitions of what constitute psychosocial interventions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThompsonSki2013_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThompsonSki2013-115">[115]</a></sup> Mental stress–induced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myocardial_ischemia" title="Myocardial ischemia">myocardial ischemia</a> is associated with an increased risk of heart problems in those with previous heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Severe emotional and physical stress leads to a form of heart dysfunction known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Takotsubo_syndrome" title="Takotsubo syndrome">Takotsubo syndrome</a> in some people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Stress, however, plays a relatively minor role in hypertension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Specific relaxation therapies are of unclear benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Relax2006_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Relax2006-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></li>
<li>Not enough sleep also raises the risk of high blood pressure. Adults need about 7–9 hours of sleep. Sleep apnea is also a major risk as it causes breathing to stop briefly, which can put stress on the body which can raise the risk of heart disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Most guidelines recommend combining preventive strategies. There is some evidence that interventions aiming to reduce more than one cardiovascular risk factor may have beneficial effects on blood pressure, body mass index and waist circumference; however, evidence was limited and the authors were unable to draw firm conclusions on the effects on cardiovascular events and mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is additional evidence to suggest that simply providing people with a cardiovascular disease risk score may reduce cardiovascular disease risk factors by a small amount compared to usual care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> However, there was some uncertainty as to whether providing these scores had any effect on cardiovascular disease events. It is unclear whether or not dental care in those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodontitis" title="Periodontitis">periodontitis</a> affects their risk of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet_2">Diet</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lipid_hypothesis" title="Lipid hypothesis">Lipid hypothesis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturated_fat_and_cardiovascular_disease" title="Saturated fat and cardiovascular disease">Saturated fat and cardiovascular disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salt_and_cardiovascular_disease" title="Salt and cardiovascular disease">Salt and cardiovascular disease</a></div>
<p>A diet high in fruits and vegetables decreases the risk of cardiovascular disease and <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2021 review found that <a href="/wiki/Plant-based_diet" title="Plant-based diet">plant-based diets</a> can provide a risk reduction for CVD if a healthy plant-based diet is consumed. Unhealthy plant-based diets do not provide benefits over diets including meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-93">[93]</a></sup> A similar meta-analysis and systematic review also looked into dietary patterns and found "that diets lower in animal foods and unhealthy plant foods, and higher in healthy plant foods are beneficial for CVD prevention".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-94">[94]</a></sup> A 2018 meta-analysis of <a href="/wiki/Observational_study" title="Observational study">observational studies</a> concluded that "In most countries, a vegan diet is associated with a more favourable cardio-metabolic profile compared to an omnivorous diet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence suggests that the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_diet" title="Mediterranean diet">Mediterranean diet</a> may improve cardiovascular outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> There is also evidence that a Mediterranean diet may be more effective than a <a href="/wiki/Low-fat_diet" title="Low-fat diet">low-fat diet</a> in bringing about long-term changes to cardiovascular risk factors (e.g., lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cholesterol_level" title="Cholesterol level">cholesterol level</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/DASH_diet" title="DASH diet">DASH diet</a> (high in nuts, fish, fruits and vegetables, and low in sweets, red meat and fat) has been shown to reduce blood pressure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> lower total and low density lipoprotein cholesterol<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>  and improve <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">metabolic syndrome</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> but the long-term benefits have been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> A high-<a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">fiber</a> diet is associated with lower risks of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Worldwide, dietary guidelines recommend a reduction in <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2013_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2013-134">[134]</a></sup> and although the role of dietary fat in cardiovascular disease is complex and controversial there is a long-standing consensus that replacing saturated fat with unsaturated fat in the diet is sound medical advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-135">[135]</a></sup> Total fat intake has not been found to be associated with cardiovascular risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Will2012_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Will2012-137">[137]</a></sup> A 2020 systematic review found moderate quality evidence that reducing saturated fat intake for at least 2 years caused a reduction in cardiovascular events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>  A 2015 meta-analysis of observational studies however did not find a convincing association between saturated fat intake and cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2015_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2015-139">[139]</a></sup>  Variation in what is used as a substitute for saturated fat may explain some differences in findings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-135">[135]</a></sup> The benefit from replacement with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fat" title="Polyunsaturated fat">polyunsaturated fats</a> appears greatest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sacks_2017_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sacks_2017-140">[140]</a></sup>  while replacement of saturated fats with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> does not appear to have a beneficial effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sacks_2017_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sacks_2017-140">[140]</a></sup> A diet high in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans_fatty_acids" title="Trans fatty acids">trans fatty acids</a> is associated with higher rates of cardiovascular disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chow2014_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chow2014-141">[141]</a></sup> and in 2015 the Food and Drug Administration (FDA) determined that there was 'no longer a consensus among qualified experts that partially hydrogenated oils (PHOs), which are the primary dietary source of industrially produced trans fatty acids (IP-TFA), are generally recognized as safe (GRAS) for any use in human food'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> There is conflicting evidence concerning whether dietary supplements of <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid" title="Omega-3 fatty acid">omega-3 fatty acids</a> (a type of polysaturated fat in oily fish) added to diet improve cardiovascular risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The benefits of recommending a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-salt_diet" title="Low-salt diet">low-salt diet</a> in people with high or normal blood pressure are not clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In those with heart failure, after one study was left out, the rest of the trials show a trend to benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He2011_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-He2011-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paterna2008_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paterna2008-147">[147]</a></sup> Another review of dietary salt concluded that there is strong evidence that high dietary salt intake increases blood pressure and worsens hypertension, and that it increases the number of cardiovascular disease events; both as a result of the increased blood pressure <i>and</i> probably through other mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bochud2011_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bochud2011-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook2007_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook2007-149">[149]</a></sup> Moderate evidence was found that high salt intake increases cardiovascular mortality; and some evidence was found for an increase in overall mortality, strokes, and <a href="/wiki/Left_ventricular_hypertrophy" title="Left ventricular hypertrophy">left ventricular hypertrophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bochud2011_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bochud2011-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intermittent_fasting">Intermittent fasting</span></h4>
<p>Overall, the current body of scientific evidence is uncertain on whether intermittent fasting could prevent cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-:52-150">[150]</a></sup> Intermittent fasting may help people lose more weight than regular eating patterns, but was not different than energy restriction diets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-:52-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medication">Medication</span></h3>
<p>Blood pressure medication reduces cardiovascular disease in people at risk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ettehad_957–967_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ettehad_957–967-110">[110]</a></sup> irrespective of age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> the baseline level of cardiovascular risk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> or baseline blood pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The commonly-used drug regimens have similar efficacy in reducing the risk of all major cardiovascular events, although there may be differences between drugs in their ability to prevent specific outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-154">[154]</a></sup> Larger reductions in blood pressure produce larger reductions in risk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-154">[154]</a></sup> and most people with high blood pressure require more than one drug to achieve adequate reduction in blood pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Adherence to medications is often poor, and while mobile phone text messaging has been tried to improve adherence, there is insufficient evidence that it alters secondary prevention of cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
